# Excalidraw Data

## Text Elements
anti parallel strands
phosphodiester backbone faces outwards
paired bases face inwards
about 10-11 bases per helix turn ^cSTqFy98

high accessibility to those in major groove
low to those in minor groove ^itgpdTi2

doesnt cut when facing inwards -> dna is on the outside of nucleosomes ^tKvp6pV8

histones are one of the most conserved proteins on this planet
yeast and human histones are 90% similar ^7gB9Emnt

scaffold refers to the protein which are not histones
 ^S7e4VqX7

RNPs are ribonucleoproteins
these are complexes formed by association of RNA and protein
these two components work together with the protein often providing a structural scaffold while the RNA component can provide specificity through base pairing or even catalytic activity acting as ribozyme ^bertiKWh

most maximum of dna condensation in tightest space possible happens in mitotic chromosomes ^I7SVhQ8U

smc protein forms a coil around itself --> coiled-coil domain

kleisin proteins are the CAPs
 ^DuXNTTgX

condensins needed to form higher order structures
 ^CAzAG89J

dissociated metaphase chromsome by coutneracting the charges holding the dna and the proteins together
 ^oN4Ib3A7

lysines and arginines on the tail of histones can be modified ^swFzdz0s

we can find out about chromosome territories by creating sequences which are complementary to the dna found in certain chromosomes
then we can create probes and tag them and identify locations of different chromosomes
distinct territories can be seen allocated to different chromosomes (this is spatial)

what about temporal?
is it dynamic? or do they stay consistent?
trace of ARS1413 locus over time in g1 phase in yeast --> there is dynamic change within a territory
theyre moving along, like socks in a washing machine!
 ^CwpH11aK

finding if seq a somewhere on the dna can interact with another seq in close proximity in the dna
we do this using chromosome conformation analysis (Hi-C) - hi resolution chromosome capture  ^PD3nTnIq

in essence, this allows us to see if two sequences (in the experiment we are specifically looking at the biotin markers
and seeing if they are in close proximity (close to each other in space or not) in 3d space ^J2a4VYFD

zones of high interaction are called TADs - topologically associated domains ^Vb9KpGIa

modelling from Hi-C ^9436LOtQ

there are functional areas for dna replication and transcription
these can be called 'factories'
 ^B3q4vE7g

replicate initially euchromatin then to heterochromatin ^n24FpcsA

dna replication loci and transcription loci rarely overlap --> division of labour


nucleoli are dynamic sites of rRNA synthesis and processing
start from many smaller sites and aggregate
 ^hGCD8Hjq

nuclear lamina (protein layer) provides physical protection of membrane system and demarcation down to chromatin
made of lamin proteins --> provide physical bag -> one side is lipids (outside) and on the other side (inner) is the chromatin ^o0EHgUkZ

last time we focused on the spatial aspect
from now we focus more on the temporal aspect

 ^PrWQz7Om

replication origins are start sites where replication begins
 ^nX6Jj4qu

replicon model - to explain conceptually how replication of circular bacterial chromosomes might be regulated
 ^wWBiRJVP

so for sv40 replication, t antigen must bind to unique site containing
inverted repeat and a conserved a/t-rich element for origin function ^zvAxJsUs

virus genomes replicate several times in one cell cycle while eukaryotic genome do so only once  ^OuAPnC9t

replicon model - explains conceptually how replication of circular bacterial chromosomes
might be regulated ^vjXP48DG

early genes - for replication of virus
late genes - for packaging ^XrcbM8fP

where we get change in strands ie from okazaki to leading strands -> where the origin is
where leading and lagging change polarity

the concept here: dna replication start at specific sites called origins of replication (ORIs).
they key insight behind sns-seq is that the very first pieces of dna synthesised at an origin are new leading strands

The bidirectionality of replication at ORI is precisely the reason for the polarity switch.


we can use modern technology to isolate these intermediates and sequence them


there are 3 key techniques that have been used 
genome-wide origin mapping techniques:
1. sequencing of short nascent strands (SNS-seq)
2. Sequencing of initiation sites (Ini-seq)
3. Sequencing of Okazaki fragments (OK-seqs) ^3Lvg0PWr

there is a RNA template (TERC) in telomerase
this is called Telomerase RNA Component. This RNA
contains a sequence that is complementary to the repeating
DHA sequence found at the ends of chromosomes (the telomeres)
 ^TeAH2mP9

this is 2 hexamers moving 
away from each other ^ySz16m8V

[[histone]]
 ^Agth1NaZ

[[MCM complex]] ^d75BeEug
